Microbiol."], "published-print": {"date-parts": [[2015, 10, 15]]}, "abstract": "<jats:title>ABSTRACT</jats:title><jats:p>Fungus-derived indole-3-acetic acid (IAA), which is involved in development of ectomycorrhiza, affects both partners, i.e., the tree and the fungus. The biosynthesis pathway, excretion from fungal hyphae, the induction of branching in fungal cultures, and enhanced Hartig net formation in mycorrhiza were shown. Gene expression studies, incorporation of labeled compounds into IAA, heterologous expression of a transporter, and bioinformatics were applied to study the effect of IAA on fungal morphogenesis and on ectomycorrhiza.<jats:named-content content-type=\"genus-species\">Tricholoma vaccinum</jats:named-content>produces IAA from tryptophan via indole-3-pyruvate, with the last step of this biosynthetic pathway being catalyzed by an aldehyde dehydrogenase. The gene<jats:italic>ald1</jats:italic>was found to be highly expressed in ectomycorrhiza and induced by indole-3-acetaldehyde. The export of IAA from fungal cells is supported by the multidrug and toxic extrusion (MATE) transporter Mte1 found in<jats:named-content content-type=\"genus-species\">T. vaccinum</jats:named-content>. The addition of IAA and its precursors induced elongated cells and hyphal ramification of mycorrhizal fungi; in contrast, in saprobic fungi such as<jats:named-content content-type=\"genus-species\">Schizophyllum commune</jats:named-content>, IAA did not induce morphogenetic changes. Mycorrhiza responded by increasing its Hartig net formation. The IAA of fungal origin acts as a diffusible signal, influencing root colonization and increasing Hartig net formation in ectomycorrhiza.</jats:p>", "DOI": "10.1128/aem.01991-15", "type":
